Social News

The Life of Working Women in New York

— Lin

(7) A Glimpse of Life

Now let us use a microscope to examine the life of working women in New York, and see how they live at home, at work, and in the vast society. To get a true picture, we must write out their daily life. Their ranks are divided by the size of their salaries. Each example is unique, but of course, there are many exceptions.

The daily life of Minnie Roschild, a woman who earns ten to fifteen dollars a week, is as follows:

"Minnie, Minnie, wake up, you lazy bones!"

"Ah, Mom, let me sleep five more minutes."

"That's what you said ten minutes ago, Minnie. If you're late, you'll be punished. You know that. The rent is due again, and I don't dare go to the butcher because I owe him a lot of money. And..."

"Okay, Mom, I'm getting up. Please stop nagging."

Minnie jumped out of her iron bed. She shared a room with her two younger sisters. There was no perfumed bath for her to take. Her two younger sisters, Rose and Connie, were already eating their breakfast. As Minnie got dressed, their conversation drifted into her ears.

"I can work next year, too. Isn't that a great thing? Maybe Minnie will take me to her store. But I want to go to Galsapp's. Isn't that a big store? Just think, I'll earn twelve dollars a month!"

Minnie hurriedly put on her shoes and stopped, saying, "Ah, Rose, don't say that. You don't know what luck is. Standing behind a counter wrapping packages all day isn't that great. You should hope to study at school for two more years."

"Minnie, your coffee is burnt," Mrs. Roschild shouted sharply.

"Oh dear, Mom, I'm late. Give me a piece of bread dipped in coffee. I can't be late today. The new supervisor is not easy to get along with."

Minnie swallowed the coffee and a large chunk of soggy bread, then put on her hat and hurried out the door, shouting, "Goodbye, Mom! Goodbye, kids!"

The Roschild family lived in a rented house on the outskirts of the city. Mr. Roschild ran a construction business. When he had work, he went out early in the morning and came home for dinner in the evening. The eldest child was twenty-four years old, already married and had a child. He worked as an assistant electrical engineer. His wife, who had once worked as a stenographer, considered herself superior to her husband's family, so Minnie was very dissatisfied with her.

Minnie took the subway alone. On the way, she thought about her sister-in-law. She wanted to buy an interesting newspaper, but she was stingy with her money. One newspaper every day would cost twelve cents a week, which was not a small amount. Secretly, Minnie envied her sister-in-law. She didn't understand why her sister-in-law had given up a twenty-five-dollar-a-week job working in a fancy office just to take care of her family and a small child.

This morning, Minnie got a seat on the subway. Usually, even if she got on the train early, she wouldn't get a seat. When the train reached the city center, she was pushed and squeezed by men and women going to work. Sometimes she got really angry, and she would poke her elbow into someone's ribs and shout, "What's going on here?" The men in the crowded subway often tried to get some fresh gossip, but Minnie never treated them silently.

Finally, she arrived at her station, with only five minutes to spare. She had to walk past three tenement buildings to get to the store. She had to punch the time clock in the cloakroom, so she ran all the way, bumping into several pedestrians. She didn't care about them. If she was late, she would have to wait an hour before she could start work. The other women were also very busy, all rushing into the employees' entrance, then going upstairs. Most of the women wore inexpensive light blue or black dresses, the two colors that the store allowed employees to wear. After punching the time clock, Minnie slowly walked to her stall. Her position was in the hosiery department on the lower floor. The hosiery department was often very busy. To Minnie, it seemed like miles of stockings passed through her hands every day. Many of the socks that were sold only needed to be put into a bag to be finished. The packaging work for other departments was much more difficult, although they didn't have to be as fast as packing socks. Minnie felt very lucky that she didn't have to pack blankets, cooking utensils, or toys. Some days, she worked like a machine in the hosiery department, and other days, she fantasized about the lives of the customers. She loved every pair of sheer French stockings. First, she folded the socks and put them in a tissue paper package, then put them in a thick paper bag. Minnie knew that one could only wear these socks once or twice, like a handful of foam. She found it strange that customers nonchalantly paid five dollars a pair for them. The buyer was probably a female singer, or maybe a real movie star. Also, there were schoolgirls who used the money they got for their birthdays to buy this luxury item. There were also many pretty customers who often made Minnie shrug. It only took a few minutes of an employee's time for women like these to buy such things. Minnie didn't care whether the customers were truly rich or just looked like it. She was just very interested in them. (To be continued)

Marshal Foch's War Diary of the European War

— Translated by Gu Angruo

The first problem to be solved was arranging for the rapid participation of French army reserves in the British army's defense zone battles, and the entry of British army reserves into the French army's zone to help in the battle. Commander-in-Chief Haig and Commander Petain had already studied this point separately, and the prepared troops were only a few divisions. Now it was time to jointly study again, and consider mobilizing all the forces of the Allied forces. This matter became the main purpose of the letters I sent to the two commanders on June 13th. This work was completed on the 20th.

Consolidating the defense lines of the British and French armies was also important. Regarding the British army's defense line, it was advocated to dispatch American troops and several British divisions to strengthen the defense.

The deployment position of the Allied forces focused on remotely protecting Paris. At the same time, in order to prevent unexpected events, a defense was arranged in the suburbs of Paris. Commander Guillaumat, who was recalled from the eastern front, was responsible for this task.

A meeting was convened at the Allied Headquarters in Bombon on June 15th. Chairman Clemenceau, (Note: The attendees were the Deputy Chairman of the Paris Defense Committee, Doumer, the Chairman of the Military Committee of the Chamber of Deputies, Renault, and Commanders Foch, Petain, Guillaumat, Degoutte, Hell, Mordacq, and Weygand, etc.) clearly divided the powers of the various military authorities.

The first step of the outline was to determine the defense of Paris. The troops designated by Commander Foch were fully responsible, step by step. The second step was to determine that if the enemy army went straight to Paris, advancing to the Manceuil-Clermont line on the Thérain River, the Paris military commander would take command of the troops defending the area between the Oise River, the Marne River, and the two river basins according to the orders of the French army commander-in-chief.

At the same time, this officer would have to prepare a defense plan, digging trenches and building fortifications in the suburbs of Paris from Antony to Noisy. The position of the garrison should take into account both defense and transportation.

For the convenience of military affairs, it was further decided to include Paris in the main battle zone. A liaison office was set up at the French army headquarters, and Commander Guillaumat was able to fully understand the situation of the French army.

The various arrangements in the front and rear were nothing more than the Allied forces' steps to meet the enemy's new offensive, and the preparation for our army's great defensive battle. But while planning to prevent the enemy from attacking, I did not forget that the Allied forces must immediately prepare to change to an offensive, because only an offensive battle can win the final victory of the war.

Two areas had already been selected for attack, and the main preliminary work had been completed by the various armies, for the British army in the area of the Lys River, and for the French army between the Oise River and the Somme River. But the German army's incursion from the Aisne River deep into the Marne River to Château-Thierry changed the situation, and a new war zone was created for the Allied forces.

In fact, it was easy to see that the enemy's isolated forces had penetrated deeply, and the railway was its only suitable transportation route, and these many routes all passed through Soissons. Therefore, if this important transportation hub was attacked by our army's artillery, "any attack by the German army in the Château-Thierry area would be in vain."

At that time, our army had already completed its deployment, and only long-range artillery could bombard Soissons. Long-range artillery fire was sufficient to affect the enemy's supply, but it could not be completely cut off. To achieve this goal, mobile heavy artillery and field artillery must also be used. Because it was necessary to deploy artillery positions, on June 14th, I asked Commander Petain to "prepare to attack and seize the high ground west of Soissons, which is a strategically important place that must not be owned by the enemy."

According to the military order of June 7th, the Fifth Army had already prepared to attack the eastern flank of Château-Thierry, but the German army's attack in the Compiègne area had greatly subsided. The enemy army had stopped in Metz, and the attack in this area was no longer important. Because of the slowdown, for this reason, I asked Commander Petain to immediately order the Tenth Army to prepare to attack, recapture the Plateau of Dommiers, and do so quickly before the enemy could calmly deploy their defenses.

Commander Petain immediately issued an order, limiting the Tenth Army to advance to "the line of Pournois-Missy-sur-Aisne." A few days later, on June 20th, the commander of the Tenth Army, Commander Mangin, drew up an operation plan, which was approved by the French army commander-in-chief.

At the end of June, the Allied forces prepared three attack locations in different areas, namely the Lys River, the Somme River, and the Marne River. Our army prepared to attack first in the third area. This plan did not hinder our army's preparation for a general offensive along the entire line, so all officers and men should prepare themselves for the opportunity to arrive.

The method of this offensive and the time of the start of the attack must be carefully selected. Since March 21st, the Allied forces had suffered many fierce battles and heavy losses. After the start of the war, the enemy had been winning, and even if there were some setbacks, they were exchanged for by the Allied forces at a great sacrifice. The strength of the enemy army was well known to everyone.

Therefore, to improve this impression, the most important thing was to win a battle. If there was a setback in the attack, our army must not be put in a dangerous situation.

The planned attack on the Soissons-Château-Thierry line should use the Aisne River to protect the left wing. If the attack was thwarted, it would not fall into the dangerous situation of an isolated army penetrating deeply with dangerous flanks.

Finally, although our army initially hoped to attack in only one place, the Allied forces' entire plan should be prepared for support, so that after a successful strike, taking advantage of the enemy's confusion, the various armies would advance separately and meet in one place. This would greatly increase the effectiveness of our army's entire operation.

After three years of trench warfare, our army should once again understand the power of action, and its practice and the sensitivity of objects. These military knowledge should be instilled in new and old officers and soldiers using a simple text.

Therefore, on June 27th, I asked Petain to issue a general order to the entire army, drawing up the outline of the offensive organization and conduct, and specifying the training time to complete the training of the French and American armies participating in this offensive.

Finally, I informed him that, at a certain stage of this offensive, I would mobilize all the troops of the French, American, and British armies. Their preparation should be completed within at least two months. The planned combat force must be at least twelve American divisions, twelve French divisions, and seven or eight British divisions.

Although the cooperation of the American army was still in its initial stage, expectations were very high. The Allied forces initially believed in the effect of the American army's participation in the war. Now this information is increasing day by day. Therefore, when the Allied forces commander-in-chief mobilizes the American army to fight, he must be careful and responsible for commanding their officers, and must always proceed according to his plan. Guidelines for Social News Submissions

(1) Articles should be about domestic and foreign science, industry, commerce, rural areas.

(2) Health, aviation, exploration, customs, or urban life news is limited to drawings of various social life lyrical styles.

(3) Photos that qualify include seasonal landscapes, animals and plants, people, and news photography. Photos of beautiful women in seasonal landscapes are especially welcome.

(4) Each article should not exceed 2,000 characters.
